Their population keeps on moving further south,” said National Atomic Energy Commission (CNEA) biologist Marianela Garcia Alba.</p></section><div class="ad__section-border article-content__ad-group They are sterilizing 10,000 males per week and aim to increase that to 500,000. They expect to release the first batch of sterilized males in November.</p><p></p><p>“They are sterilized through ionizing energy and those sterile males are freed into the fields and when they meet with a wild female, their offspring are not viable,” said Garcia Alba. “This way, by successive release of such males we’ll be able to reduce the population of the vector mosquito.”</p><p></p><p>Dengue is transmitted through the bites of aedes aegypti mosquitoes. Its symptoms include fever, eye, head, muscle and joint pain, nausea, vomiting and fatigue.</p><p></p><p>Similar techniques to sterilize pests using the same radiation found in X-rays have been utilized for decades, helping global efforts to control diseases such as chikungunya, dengue and Zika.</p><p></p><p>(Reporting by Horacio Soria and Juan Bustamente; Writing by Adam Jourdan, editing by Ed Osmond)</p><p></p><p data-async=""></p><p><em>For more health news and content around diseases, conditions, wellness, healthy living, drugs, treatments and more, head to <a
